<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Hey Jude</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p>"<b>Hey Jude</b>" is a song by the English <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a> band <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>, written by <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a> and credited to <a href="/wiki/Lennon%E2%80%93McCartney" title="Lennon–McCartney">Lennon–McCartney</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ballad" title="Ballad">ballad</a> evolved from "Hey Jules", a song McCartney wrote to comfort <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>'s son, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Lennon" title="Julian Lennon">Julian</a>, during his parents' divorce. "Hey Jude" begins with a verse-bridge structure incorporating McCartney's vocal performance and piano accompaniment; further instrumentation is added as the song progresses. After the fourth verse, the song shifts to a fade-out <a href="/wiki/Coda_(music)" title="Coda (music)">coda</a> that lasts for more than four minutes.</p>
<p>"Hey Jude" was released in August 1968 as the first single from the Beatles' record label <a href="/wiki/Apple_Records" title="Apple Records">Apple Records</a>. More than seven minutes in length, it was at the time the longest single ever to top the British charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELowry200244_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELowry200244-1">[1]</a></sup> It also spent nine weeks at number one in the United States, the longest for any Beatles single. "Hey Jude" tied the "all-time" record, at the time, for the longest run at the top of the US charts. The single has sold approximately eight million copies and is frequently included on professional critics' lists of the greatest songs of all time. In 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> named it the 10th biggest song of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bronson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bronson-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inspiration_and_writing">Inspiration and writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Inspiration and writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In May 1968,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011397_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011397-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Cynthia_Lennon" title="Cynthia Lennon">Cynthia Lennon</a> separated because of John's affair with <a href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono" title="Yoko Ono">Yoko Ono</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389-4">[4]</a></sup> The following month, <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a> drove out to visit Cynthia and John's son, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Lennon" title="Julian Lennon">Julian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Kenwood,_St._George%27s_Hill" title="Kenwood, St. George's Hill">Kenwood</a>, the family's home in <a href="/wiki/Weybridge" title="Weybridge">Weybridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESounes2010208.2C_221_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESounes2010208.2C_221-6">[6]</a></sup> Cynthia had been part of <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>' social circle since before the band's rise to fame in 1963;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESounes2010221_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESounes2010221-7">[7]</a></sup> McCartney later said he found it "a bit much for them suddenly to be <i><a href="/wiki/Persona_non_grata" title="Persona non grata">personae non gratae</a></i> and out of my life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997465_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997465-8">[8]</a></sup> Cynthia Lennon recalled of McCartney's surprise visit: "I was touched by his obvious concern for our welfare ... On the journey down he composed 'Hey Jude' in the car. I will never forget Paul's gesture of care and concern in coming to see us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005366_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005366-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9; padding:8px;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>I started with the idea "Hey Jules," which was Julian, don't make it bad, take a sad song and make it better. Hey, try and deal with this terrible thing. I knew it was not going to be easy for him. I always feel sorry for kids in divorces...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997465_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997465-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">– <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>, 1997</cite></div>
<p>The song's original title was "Hey Jules", and it was intended to comfort Julian Lennon from the stress of his parents' separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389-4">[4]</a></sup> McCartney later said, "I knew it was not going to be easy for him", and that he changed the name to "Jude" "because I thought that sounded a bit better".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997465_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997465-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to music journalist Chris Hunt, in the weeks after writing the song, McCartney "test[ed] his latest composition on anyone too polite to refuse. And that meant <i>everyone</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunt_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-10">[10]</a></sup> On 30 June, after recording the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Dyke_Mills_Band" title="Black Dyke Mills Band">Black Dyke Mills Band</a>'s rendition of his instrumental "Thingumybob", in <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>, McCartney stopped at a village in <a href="/wiki/Bedfordshire" title="Bedfordshire">Bedfordshire</a> and performed "Hey Jude" at a local pub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001302_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001302-11">[11]</a></sup> He also regaled members of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Bonzo_Dog_Band" title="The Bonzo Dog Band">the Bonzo Dog Band</a> with the song while producing their single "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_the_Urban_Spaceman" title="I'm the Urban Spaceman">I'm the Urban Spaceman</a>", in London, and interrupted a recording session by <a href="/wiki/The_Barron_Knights" title="The Barron Knights">the Barron Knights</a> to do the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunt_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-10">[10]</a></sup> Ron Griffith of the group the Iveys – soon to be known as <a href="/wiki/Badfinger" title="Badfinger">Badfinger</a> and, like the Black Dyke Mills Band, an early signing to the Beatles' new record label <a href="/wiki/Apple_Records" title="Apple Records">Apple Records</a> – later recalled that on their first day in the studio, McCartney "gave us a full concert rendition of 'Hey Jude'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005367_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005367-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[nb 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>When introducing the composition to Lennon, McCartney assured him that he would "fix" the line "the movement you need is on your shoulder", reasoning that "it's a stupid expression; it sounds like a parrot." Lennon replied: "You won't, you know. That's the best line in the song."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000297_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000297-14">[13]</a></sup> McCartney retained the phrase;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389-4">[4]</a></sup> he later said of his subsequent live performances of the song: "that's the line when I think of John, and sometimes I get a little emotional during that moment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000297_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000297-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9; padding:8px;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>If you think about it ... Yoko's just come into the picture. He's saying. "Hey, Jude – Hey, John." I know I'm sounding like one of those fans who reads things into it, but you can <i>hear</i> it as a song to me ... Subconsciously, he was saying, Go ahead, leave me. On a conscious level, he didn't want me to go ahead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheff2000186_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheff2000186-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">– <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>, 1980</cite></div>
<p>Although McCartney originally wrote "Hey Jude" for Julian, John Lennon thought it had actually been written for him. In a 1980 interview, Lennon stated that he "always heard it as a song to me" and contended that, on one level, McCartney was giving his blessing to Lennon and Ono's relationship, while, on another, he was disappointed to be usurped as Lennon's friend and songwriting partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheff2000186_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheff2000186-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other people believed McCartney wrote the song about them, including Judith Simons, a journalist with the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Still others, including Lennon, have speculated that in the lyrics to "Hey Jude", McCartney's failing long-term relationship with <a href="/wiki/Jane_Asher" title="Jane Asher">Jane Asher</a> provided an unconscious "message to himself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusic_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusic-17">[16]</a></sup> McCartney and Asher had announced their engagement on 25 December 1967,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrTyler197870_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrTyler197870-18">[17]</a></sup> yet he began an affair with <a href="/wiki/Linda_McCartney" title="Linda McCartney">Linda Eastman</a> in June 1968;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESounes2010213.E2.80.9314_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESounes2010213.E2.80.9314-19">[18]</a></sup> that same month, <a href="/wiki/Francie_Schwartz" title="Francie Schwartz">Francie Schwartz</a>, an American who was in London to discuss a film proposal with Apple, began living with McCartney at his <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s_Wood" title="St John's Wood">St John's Wood</a> home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201147.E2.80.9348_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201147.E2.80.9348-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESounes2010212.E2.80.9313.2C_215_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESounes2010212.E2.80.9313.2C_215-21">[20]</a></sup> When Lennon mentioned that he thought the song was about him and Ono, McCartney denied it and told Lennon he had written the song about himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1995236_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1995236-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[nb 2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Author <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hertsgaard" title="Mark Hertsgaard">Mark Hertsgaard</a> has commented that "many of the song's lyrics do seem directed more at a grown man on the verge of a powerful new love, especially the lines 'you have found her now go and get her' and 'you're waiting for someone to perform with.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1995236_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1995236-22">[21]</a></sup> Music critic and author <a href="/wiki/Tim_Riley_(music_critic)" title="Tim Riley (music critic)">Tim Riley</a> writes: "If the song is about self-worth and self-consolation in the face of hardship, the vocal performance itself conveys much of the journey. He begins by singing to comfort someone else, finds himself weighing his own feelings in the process, and finally, in the repeated refrains that nurture his own approbation, he comes to believe in himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2002255_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2002255-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbey_Road_rehearsals">Abbey Road rehearsals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Abbey Road rehearsals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Having earmarked the song for release as a single, the Beatles recorded "Hey Jude" during the sessions for their <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_(album)" title="The Beatles (album)">self-titled double album</a>, commonly known as "the White Album".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988145_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988145-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005782_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005782-27">[25]</a></sup> The sessions were marked by an element of discord within the group for the first time, partly as a result of Ono's constant presence at Lennon's side,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1996247.2C_251_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1996247.2C_251-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201144.E2.80.9345_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201144.E2.80.9345-29">[27]</a></sup> and also reflective of the four band members' divergence following their <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_in_India" title="The Beatles in India">communal trip to Rishikesh</a> in the spring of 1968 to study <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation" title="Transcendental Meditation">Transcendental Meditation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner197888.E2.80.9389_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner197888.E2.80.9389-30">[28]</a></sup> Author <a href="/wiki/Peter_Doggett" title="Peter Doggett">Peter Doggett</a> describes the completed version of "Hey Jude" as a song that "glowed with optimism after a summer that had burned with anxiety and rage within the group".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201149.E2.80.9350_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201149.E2.80.9350-31">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Beatles first taped 25 <a href="/wiki/Take" title="Take">takes</a> of the song at <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road_Studios" title="Abbey Road Studios">Abbey Road Studios</a> in London over two nights, 29 and 30 July 1968,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988145_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988145-26">[24]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/George_Martin" title="George Martin">George Martin</a> as their producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389.E2.80.9390_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014389.E2.80.9390-32">[30]</a></sup> These dates served as rehearsals, however, since they planned to record the master track at <a href="/wiki/Trident_Studios" title="Trident Studios">Trident Studios</a> to utilise their <a href="/wiki/Multitrack_recording" title="Multitrack recording">eight-track recording machine</a> (Abbey Road was still limited to four-tracks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988145_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988145-26">[24]</a></sup> A take from 29 July, which author and critic <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Womack" title="Kenneth Womack">Kenneth Womack</a> describes as a "jovial" session,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014390_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014390-33">[31]</a></sup> was issued on the <i><a href="/wiki/Anthology_3" title="Anthology 3">Anthology 3</a></i> compilation in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 30 July rehearsals were filmed for a short documentary titled <i>Music!</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001304_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001304-35">[33]</a></sup> The film shows only three of the Beatles performing "Hey Jude", however, since <a href="/wiki/George_Harrison" title="George Harrison">George Harrison</a> remained in the studio control room,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194-36">[34]</a></sup> with Martin and EMI recording engineer <a href="/wiki/Ken_Scott" title="Ken Scott">Ken Scott</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011407_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011407-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[nb 3]</a></sup> Author Simon Leng views this as indicative of how Harrison was increasingly allowed little room to develop ideas on McCartney compositions, whereas he was free to create empathetic guitar parts for Lennon's songs of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeng200634.E2.80.9335_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeng200634.E2.80.9335-40">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the rehearsals that day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011407_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011407-37">[35]</a></sup> Harrison and McCartney had a heated disagreement over the lead guitar part for the song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014390_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014390-33">[31]</a></sup> Harrison's idea was to play a guitar phrase as a response to each line of the vocal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000316_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000316-41">[38]</a></sup> which did not fit with McCartney's conception of the song's arrangement, and he vetoed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146-42">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997466_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997466-43">[40]</a></sup> In a 1994 interview, McCartney said, "looking back on it, I think, Okay. Well, it was bossy, but it was ballsy of me, because I could have bowed to the pressure."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997466_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997466-43">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ron_Richards_(producer)" title="Ron Richards (producer)">Ron Richards</a>, a record producer who worked for Martin at both <a href="/wiki/Parlophone" title="Parlophone">Parlophone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Associated_Independent_Recording" title="Associated Independent Recording">AIR Studios</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup> said McCartney was "oblivious to anyone else's feelings in the studio", and that he was driven to making the best possible record, at almost any cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpitz2005783_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitz2005783-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[nb 4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trident_Studios_recording">Trident Studios recording</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Trident Studios recording">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The master track for "Hey Jude" was recorded at Trident Studios on 31 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001304_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001304-35">[33]</a></sup> Trident's founder, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Sheffield" title="Norman Sheffield">Norman Sheffield</a>, recalled that <a href="/wiki/Mal_Evans" title="Mal Evans">Mal Evans</a>, the Beatles' aide and former roadie, insisted that some pot plants he had brought be placed in the studio to make the place "soft", consistent with the band's wishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield201315_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield201315-50">[46]</a></sup> Sheffield served as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recording_engineer" title="Recording engineer">recording engineer</a> for the session. The line-up on the basic track was McCartney on piano and lead vocal, Lennon on acoustic guitar, Harrison on electric guitar, and <a href="/wiki/Ringo_Starr" title="Ringo Starr">Ringo Starr</a> on drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146-42">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Beatles recorded four takes of "Hey Jude", the first of which was selected as the master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146-42">[39]</a></sup> With drums absent for the first 50 seconds of the song, McCartney began this take unaware that Starr had just left for a toilet break. Starr soon returned – "tiptoeing past my back rather quickly", in McCartney's recollection – and performed his cue perfectly. McCartney added: "his timing was absolutely impeccable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles1997466_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles1997466-43">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 August, the group carried out <a href="/wiki/Overdubbing" title="Overdubbing">overdubs</a> on the basic track, again at Trident. These additions included McCartney's lead vocal and bass guitar; backing vocals from Lennon, McCartney and Harrison; and tambourine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194-36">[34]</a></sup> played by Starr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998264_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998264-51">[47]</a></sup> They then added a 36-piece orchestra over the long <a href="/wiki/Coda_(music)" title="Coda (music)">coda</a>, scored by Martin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146-42">[39]</a></sup> The orchestra consisted of ten violins, three violas, three cellos, two flutes, one contra bassoon, one bassoon, two clarinets, one contra bass clarinet, four trumpets, four trombones, two horns, percussion and two string basses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998264_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998264-51">[47]</a></sup> With the introduction of what musicologist <a href="/wiki/Walter_Everett_(musicologist)" title="Walter Everett (musicologist)">Walter Everett</a> terms the "bottom-heavy" orchestral instruments, particularly the string basses, McCartney's bass part was cut from the start of the coda onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194.E2.80.9395_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194.E2.80.9395-52">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Sheffield, there was dissension initially among the orchestral musicians, some of whom "were looking down their noses at the Beatles, I think". Sheffield recalls that McCartney ensured their cooperation by demanding: "Do you guys want to get fucking paid or not?" During the first few takes, McCartney was unhappy about the lack of energy and passion in the orchestra's performance, so he stood up on the grand piano and started conducting the musicians from there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield201318_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield201318-53">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Beatles then asked the orchestra members if they would clap their hands and sing along to the refrain in the coda. All but one of the musicians complied (for a double fee), with the abstainer reportedly saying, "I'm not going to clap my hands and sing Paul McCartney's bloody song!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146-42">[39]</a></sup> Apple Records assistant Chris O'Dell says she joined the cast of backing singers on the song;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Dell200974.E2.80.9375_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Dell200974.E2.80.9375-54">[50]</a></sup> one of the label's first signings, <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Lomax" title="Jackie Lomax">Jackie Lomax</a>, also recalled participating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trident was paid £25 per hour by EMI for the "Hey Jude" sessions. Sheffield said that the studio earned about £1000 in total at the time, but by having the Beatles record there, and in turn raving about the facility, the value was incalculable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield201320_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield201320-56">[52]</a></sup> The band carried out further work at Trident during 1968,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001308.2C_311_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001308.2C_311-57">[53]</a></sup> and Apple artists such as Lomax, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Hopkin" title="Mary Hopkin">Mary Hopkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Preston" title="Billy Preston">Billy Preston</a> and the Iveys all recorded there over the next year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Dell200973.2C_92_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Dell200973.2C_92-58">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[nb 5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixing">Mixing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mixing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scott, Martin and the Beatles mixed the finished recording at Abbey Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunt_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-10">[10]</a></sup> The transfer of the Trident master tape to <a href="/wiki/Acetate_disc" title="Acetate disc">acetate</a> proved problematic due to the recording sounding murky when played back on EMI's equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunt_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-10">[10]</a></sup> The issue was resolved with the help of <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Emerick" title="Geoff Emerick">Geoff Emerick</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011408_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011408-60">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Times-61">[56]</a></sup> whom Scott had recently replaced as the Beatles' principal recording engineer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup> Emerick happened to be visiting Abbey Road,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011407.E2.80.9308_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011407.E2.80.9308-63">[58]</a></sup> having recently refused to work with the Beatles any longer, due to the tension and abuse that had become commonplace at their recording sessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988143_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988143-64">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001303_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001303-65">[60]</a></sup> A stereo mix of "Hey Jude" was then completed on 2 August and the mono version on 8 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999195_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999195-66">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[nb 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Everett writes that the song's "most commented-on feature" is its considerable length, at 7:11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999192_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999192-69">[63]</a></sup> The precedent for issuing such a long track on a single had recently been set by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Harris" title="Richard Harris">Richard Harris</a>' hit recording of "<a href="/wiki/MacArthur_Park_(song)" title="MacArthur Park (song)">MacArthur Park</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup> the composer of which, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Webb" title="Jimmy Webb">Jimmy Webb</a>, was a visitor to the studio around this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998266fn_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998266fn-71">[65]</a></sup> According to Webb, Martin admitted to him that "Hey Jude" was only allowed to run over seven minutes because of the success of "MacArthur Park".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the song's final bridge section, at 2:58,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391-73">[67]</a></sup> the spoken phrase "Fucking hell!" appears. Scott admits that although he was told about it, he could not hear the words originally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunt_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-10">[10]</a></sup> Lennon attributed the expletive to McCartney, according to Emerick,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Times-61">[56]</a></sup> who reports Lennon's comment in his autobiography: "'Paul hit a clunker on the piano and said a naughty word,' Lennon gleefully crowed, 'but I insisted we leave it in [at Trident], buried just low enough so that it can barely be heard. Most people won't ever spot it ... but we'll know it's there.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006263_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEmerickMassey2006263-74">[68]</a></sup> Womack considers that the expletive was actually uttered by Lennon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391-73">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Toft" title="Malcolm Toft">Malcolm Toft</a>, the mix engineer on the Trident recording,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[69]</a></sup> also attributes it to Lennon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyanKehew2006288_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyanKehew2006288-76">[70]</a></sup> In Toft's recollection, Lennon was overdubbing his harmony vocal when, in reaction to the volume being too loud in his headphones, he first called out "Whoa!" then, two seconds later, swore as he pulled the headphones off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyanKehew2006288.E2.80.9389_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyanKehew2006288.E2.80.9389-77">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[nb 7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition_and_structure">Composition and structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Composition and structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:147px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HeyJude68sheet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="325" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/HeyJude68sheet.jpg/145px-HeyJude68sheet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/HeyJude68sheet.jpg/218px-HeyJude68sheet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/HeyJude68sheet.jpg/290px-HeyJude68sheet.jpg 2x" width="145"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HeyJude68sheet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1968 sheet music cover (Maclen Music, Inc., New York)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"Hey Jude" begins with McCartney singing lead vocals and playing the piano. The patterns he plays are based on three chords: F, C, and B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;">♭</span> (I, V and IV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELowry200244_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELowry200244-1">[1]</a></sup> The main chord progression is "flipped on its head", in Hertsgaard's words, for the coda, since the C chord is replaced by E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;">♭</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1995249.E2.80.93250_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1995249.E2.80.93250-79">[72]</a></sup> Everett comments that McCartney's melody over the verses borrows in part from <a href="/wiki/John_Ireland_(composer)" title="John Ireland (composer)">John Ireland</a>'s 1907 <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_music" title="Liturgical music">liturgical</a> piece <i>Te Deum</i>, as well as (with the first change to a B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;">♭</span> chord) suggesting the influence of <a href="/wiki/The_Drifters" title="The Drifters">the Drifters</a>' 1960 hit "<a href="/wiki/Save_the_Last_Dance_for_Me" title="Save the Last Dance for Me">Save the Last Dance for Me</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999192_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999192-69">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second verse of the song adds accompaniment from acoustic guitar and tambourine. Tim Riley writes that, with the "restrained tom-tom and cymbal fill" that introduces the drum part, "the piano shifts downward to add a flat seventh to the tonic chord, making the downbeat of the bridge the point of arrival ('And any time you feel the <i>pain</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2002252_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2002252-80">[73]</a></sup> At the end of each bridge, McCartney sings a brief phrase ("Na-na-na na …"), supported by an electric guitar fill,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999194-36">[34]</a></sup> before playing a piano fill that leads to the next verse. According to Riley, this vocal phrase serves to "reorient the harmony for the verse as the piano figure turns upside down into a vocal aside". Additional musical details, such as tambourine on the third verse and subtle harmonies accompanying the lead vocal, are added to sustain interest throughout the four-verse, two-bridge song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2002253_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2002253-81">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The verse-bridge structure persists for approximately three minutes, after which the band leads into a four-minute-long coda, consisting of nineteen rounds of the song's double <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plagal_cadence" title="Plagal cadence">plagal cadence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverett1999192_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverett1999192-69">[63]</a></sup> During this coda, the rest of the band, backed by an orchestra that also provides backing vocals, repeats the phrase "Na-na-na na" followed by the words "hey Jude" until the song gradually fades out. In his analysis of the composition, musicologist <a href="/wiki/Alan_W._Pollack" title="Alan W. Pollack">Alan Pollack</a> comments on the unusual structure of "Hey Jude", in that it uses a "binary form that combines a fully developed, hymn-like song together with an extended, <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a>-like jam on a simple chord progression".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollack_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pollack-82">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Riley considers that the coda's repeated chord sequence (I-<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;">♭</span>VII-IV-I) "answers all the musical questions raised at the beginnings and ends of bridges", since "The flat seventh that posed dominant turns into bridges now has an entire chord built on it." This three-chord refrain allows McCartney "a bedding ... to leap about on vocally",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2002254_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2002254-83">[76]</a></sup> so he ad-libs his vocal performance for the rest of the song. In Riley's estimation, the song "becomes a tour of Paul's vocal range: from the graceful inviting tones of the opening verse, through the mounting excitement of the song itself, to the surging raves of the coda".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2002255_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2002255-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9; padding:8px;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>[The Beatles] are confident and cheerful and the human condition will be thrilled by the coming results of their willing and enduring Beatle bondage … they will give all of us new wonders to soothe our pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201149.2C_359_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201149.2C_359-84">[77]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">– <a href="/wiki/Derek_Taylor" title="Derek Taylor">Derek Taylor</a>, "Hey Jude" press release, August 1968</cite></div>
<p>"Hey Jude" was released on 26 August 1968 in the United States and 30 August in the United Kingdom,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik197667.E2.80.9368_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik197667.E2.80.9368-85">[78]</a></sup> backed with "<a href="/wiki/Revolution_(Beatles_song)" title="Revolution (Beatles song)">Revolution</a>" on the <a href="/wiki/A-side_and_B-side" title="A-side and B-side">B-side</a> of a 7" single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988200_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988200-86">[79]</a></sup> It was one of four singles issued simultaneously to launch Apple Records – the others being Mary Hopkin's "<a href="/wiki/Those_Were_the_Days_(song)" title="Those Were the Days (song)">Those Were the Days</a>", Jackie Lomax's "<a href="/wiki/Sour_Milk_Sea" title="Sour Milk Sea">Sour Milk Sea</a>", and the Black Dyke Mills Band's "Thingumybob".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306-87">[80]</a></sup> In advance of the release date, Apple declared 11–18 August to be "National Apple Week" in the UK,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306-87">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[81]</a></sup> and sent gift-wrapped boxes of the records, marked "Our First Four", to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> and other members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" title="British Royal Family">royal family</a>, and to the British prime minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner1978111_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner1978111-89">[82]</a></sup> The release was promoted by <a href="/wiki/Derek_Taylor" title="Derek Taylor">Derek Taylor</a>, who, in Doggett's description, "hyped the first Apple records with typical elan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201149_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201149-90">[83]</a></sup> "Hey Jude" was the first of the four singles, since it was still designated as an EMI/Parlophone release in the UK and a <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Records" title="Capitol Records">Capitol</a> release in the US, but with the Apple Records logo now added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESounes2010223_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESounes2010223-91">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[nb 8]</a></sup> In the US, "Hey Jude" was the first Beatles single to be issued in a company sleeve rather than a picture sleeve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lennon wanted "Revolution" to be the A-side of the single, but the other Beatles did not agree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011406.2C_408_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011406.2C_408-95">[87]</a></sup> In his 1970 interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>, he said "Hey Jude" was worthy of an A-side, "but we could have had both."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[88]</a></sup> In 1980, he told <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i> he still disagreed with the decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheff2000187_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheff2000187-97">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>The single was a highly successful debut for Apple Records,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoggett201152_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoggett201152-98">[90]</a></sup> and contrasted with the public embarrassment the band faced after the recent closure of their short-lived retail venture, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Boutique" title="Apple Boutique">Apple Boutique</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011408_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011408-60">[55]</a></sup> "Hey Jude" began its sixteen-week run on Britain's <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">official singles chart</a> on 7 September 1968, claiming the top spot a week later. It lasted two weeks on top before being replaced by Hopkin's "Those Were the Days", which was produced by McCartney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005481_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005481-99">[91]</a></sup> "Hey Jude" was certified <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">gold</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> (RIAA) on 13 September; that same week, <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> reported that two million copies of the single had been sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchultheiss1980220_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchultheiss1980220-100">[92]</a></sup> The song entered the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> in the US on 14 September, beginning a nineteen-week chart run there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik1976350_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik1976350-101">[93]</a></sup> It reached number one on 28 September and held that position for nine weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005539_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005539-102">[94]</a></sup> for three of which, "Those Were the Days" held the number-two spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik1976350_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECastlemanPodrazik1976350-101">[93]</a></sup> This was the longest time spent by a Beatles single at number one,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005604.E2.80.9305_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005604.E2.80.9305-103">[95]</a></sup> as well as being the longest-playing single to reach number one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005539_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005539-102">[94]</a></sup> The song was the 16th number-one hit for the band in America, tying <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>'s record at the time for most number-one songs there. <i>Billboard</i> ranked it as the number-one song for 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005539_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005539-102">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the UK, where "MacArthur Park" had failed to top the chart, "Hey Jude" remained the longest number-one hit for nearly a quarter of a century. It was surpassed in 1993 by <a href="/wiki/Meat_Loaf" title="Meat Loaf">Meat Loaf</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/I%27d_Do_Anything_for_Love_(But_I_Won%27t_Do_That)" title="I'd Do Anything for Love (But I Won't Do That)">I'd Do Anything for Love (But I Won't Do That)</a>", which ran to 7:52 as a single.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 30 November 1968, <i>NME</i> reported that sales had reached nearly six million copies worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchultheiss1980226_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchultheiss1980226-105">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001321_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001321-106">[98]</a></sup> "Hey Jude" became the biggest-selling debut release for a record label ever, selling an estimated eight million copies worldwide and topping the charts in eleven countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005481_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005481-99">[91]</a></sup> In 1999, it was certified <a href="/wiki/RIAA_certification" title="RIAA certification">4x platinum</a> by the RIAA, representing four million units shipped in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Promotion">Promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A failed early promotional attempt for the single took place after the Beatles' all-night recording session on 7–8 August 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001305.E2.80.9306_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001305.E2.80.9306-108">[100]</a></sup> With Apple Boutique having closed a week before, McCartney and his girlfriend, Francie Schwartz, painted <i>Hey Jude/Revolution</i> across its large, <a href="/wiki/Whitewash" title="Whitewash">whitewashed</a> shop windows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESounes2010222_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESounes2010222-109">[101]</a></sup> The words were mistaken for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" title="Anti-Semitism">anti-Semitic</a> graffiti (since <i>Jude</i> means "Jew" in German),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESounes2010222_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESounes2010222-109">[101]</a></sup> leading to complaints from the local Jewish community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306-87">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391.E2.80.9392_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391.E2.80.9392-110">[102]</a></sup> and the windows being smashed by passers-by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> Discussing the episode in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_Anthology_(book)" title="The Beatles Anthology (book)">The Beatles Anthology</a></i>, McCartney explained that he had been motivated by the location – "Great opportunity. <a href="/wiki/Baker_Street" title="Baker Street">Baker Street</a>, millions of buses going around …" – and added: "I had no idea it meant 'Jew', but if you look at footage of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span><i>Juden Raus</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> was written in whitewashed windows with a <a href="/wiki/Star_of_David" title="Star of David">Star of David</a>. I swear it never occurred to me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000297_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Beatles2000297-14">[13]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Barry_Miles" title="Barry Miles">Barry Miles</a>, McCartney caused further controversy in his comments to Alan Smith of the <i>NME</i> that month when he said: "Starvation in India doesn't worry me one bit, not one iota … And it doesn't worry you, if you're honest. You just pose."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001306-87">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[nb 9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Beatles hired <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lindsay-Hogg" title="Michael Lindsay-Hogg">Michael Lindsay-Hogg</a> to shoot a promotional film for "Hey Jude", after he had previously directed a clip for "<a href="/wiki/Paperback_Writer" title="Paperback Writer">Paperback Writer</a>" in 1966. They settled on the idea of filming with a live, albeit controlled audience. In the film, the Beatles are first seen by themselves, performing the initial chorus and verses, and then are joined by the audience who appear as the last chorus concludes and coda begins; the audience sings and claps along with the Beatles through the song's conclusion. Hogg shot the film at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twickenham_Film_Studios" title="Twickenham Film Studios">Twickenham Film Studios</a> on 4 September 1968, with McCartney himself designing the set. Tony Bramwell, a friend of the Beatles, later described the set as "the piano, there; drums, there; and orchestra in two tiers at the back."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988151_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988151-113">[104]</a></sup> The event marked Starr's return to the group, after McCartney's criticism of his drumming had led to him walking out during a session for the White Album track "<a href="/wiki/Back_in_the_U.S.S.R." title="Back in the U.S.S.R.">Back in the U.S.S.R.</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1996250.E2.80.9351_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1996250.E2.80.9351-114">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClayson2003183.E2.80.9384_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClayson2003183.E2.80.9384-115">[106]</a></sup> During his two-week absence, Starr announced that he had left the band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988151_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988151-113">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>The final film was a combination of several different takes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinchabout_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinchabout-116">[107]</a></sup> and included filmed "introductions" to the song by <a href="/wiki/David_Frost" title="David Frost">David Frost</a> (who introduced the Beatles as "the greatest tea-room orchestra in the world"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005368_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005368-117">[108]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Richard" title="Cliff Richard">Cliff Richard</a>, for their respective, eponymous TV programmes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinchabout_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pinchabout-116">[107]</a></sup> As filming wore on, Lennon repeatedly asked Lindsay-Hogg if he had the footage he needed. After 12 takes, McCartney said, "I think that's enough", and filming concluded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinchabout_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pinchabout-116">[107]</a></sup> It was first aired in the UK on <i>Frost on Sunday</i> on 8 September 1968,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001309_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001309-118">[109]</a></sup> and the film was later broadcast for the United States on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Smothers_Brothers_Comedy_Hour" title="The Smothers Brothers Comedy Hour">The Smothers Brothers Comedy Hour</a></i> on 6 October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchultheiss1980222_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchultheiss1980222-119">[110]</a></sup> According to Riley, the showing on <i>Frost on Sunday</i> "kicked 'Hey Jude' into the stratosphere" in terms of popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiley2011412_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiley2011412-120">[111]</a></sup> Hertsgaard pairs it with the release of the animated film <i><a href="/wiki/Yellow_Submarine_(film)" title="Yellow Submarine (film)">Yellow Submarine</a></i> as two events that created "a state of nirvana" for Beatles fans, in contrast with the problems besetting the band regarding Ono's influence and Apple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1996247.2C_250_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHertsgaard1996247.2C_250-121">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 4 September 1968 promo film is included in the Beatles' 2015 video compilation <i><a href="/wiki/1_(Beatles_album)" title="1 (Beatles album)">1</a></i>, while the three-disc versions of that compilation, titled <i>1+</i>, also include an alternate video, with a different introduction and vocal, from the same date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Upon the single's release, Derek Johnson of the <i>NME</i> wrote: "The intriguing features of 'Hey Jude' are its extreme length and the 40-piece orchestral accompaniment – and personally I would have preferred it without either!" While he viewed the track overall as "a beautiful, compelling song", and the first three minutes as "absolutely sensational", Johnson rued the long coda's "vocal improvisations on the basically repetitive four-bar chorus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[114]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine described the coda as "a fadeout that engagingly spoofs the fadeout as a gimmick for ending pop records". The same reviewer contrasted "Hey Jude" with its B-side, "Revolution", saying that "The other side of the new disk urges activism of a different sort", due to McCartney "liltingly exhort[ing] a friend to overcome his fears and commit himself in love".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time-124">[115]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> also attributed the song's meaning as a message from McCartney to Lennon to end his negative relationships with women: "to break the old pattern; to really go through with love".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[116]</a></sup> Other commentators interpreted "Hey Jude" as being directed at <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a>, then semi-retired in <a href="/wiki/Woodstock,_New_York" title="Woodstock, New York">Woodstock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998266_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998266-126">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrTyler197873_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrTyler197873-127">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writing in 1971, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i> called it "one of [McCartney's] truest and most forthright love songs" and was critical of its omission from the album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_(album)" title="The Beatles (album)">The Beatles</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[119]</a></sup> In their 1975 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles:_An_Illustrated_Record" title="The Beatles: An Illustrated Record">The Beatles: An Illustrated Record</a></i>, critics <a href="/wiki/Roy_Carr" title="Roy Carr">Roy Carr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tony_Tyler" title="Tony Tyler">Tony Tyler</a> wrote that "Hey Jude" "promised great things" for the ill-conceived Apple enterprise and described the song as "the last great Beatles single recorded <i>specifically</i> for the 45s market". They noted also that "the epic proportions of the piece" encouraged many imitators, yet these other artists "[failed] to capture the gentleness and sympathy of the Beatles' communal feel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrTyler197873_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrTyler197873-127">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among more recent commentators, Alan Pollack admires "Hey Jude" as "such a good illustration of two compositional lessons – how to fill a large canvas with simple means, and how to use diverse elements such as harmony, bassline, and orchestration to articulate form and contrast."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollack_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pollack-82">[75]</a></sup> Pollack considers that the song's long coda provides "an astonishingly transcendental effect",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollack_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pollack-82">[75]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Richie_Unterberger" title="Richie Unterberger">Richie Unterberger</a> similarly opines: "What could have very easily been boring is instead hypnotic because McCartney varies the vocal with some of the greatest nonsense scatting ever heard in rock …"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusic_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusic-17">[16]</a></sup> In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Revolution_in_the_Head" title="Revolution in the Head">Revolution in the Head</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Ian_MacDonald" title="Ian MacDonald">Ian MacDonald</a> wrote that the "pseudo-soul shrieking in the fade-out may be a blemish" but he praised the song as "a pop/rock hybrid drawing on the best of both idioms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998265_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998265-129">[120]</a></sup> MacDonald concluded: "'Hey Jude' strikes a universal note, touching on an archetypal moment in male sexual psychology with a gentle wisdom one might properly call inspired."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998266_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald1998266-126">[117]</a></sup> Lennon said the song was "one of [McCartney's] masterpieces".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheff2000186_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheff2000186-15">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_accolades">Awards and accolades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Awards and accolades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Hey Jude" was nominated for the <a href="/wiki/11th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="11th Annual Grammy Awards">Grammy Awards of 1969</a> in the categories of <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Record_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Record of the Year">Record of the Year</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Song_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Song of the Year">Song of the Year</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Pop_Performance_by_a_Duo_or_Group_with_Vocals" title="Grammy Award for Best Pop Performance by a Duo or Group with Vocals">Best Pop Performance by a Duo or Group with Vocal</a>, but failed to win any of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[121]</a></sup> In the 1968 <i>NME</i> Readers' Poll, "Hey Jude" was named the best single of the year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiles2001322_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiles2001322-131">[122]</a></sup> and the song also won the 1968 <a href="/wiki/Ivor_Novello_Awards" title="Ivor Novello Awards">Ivor Novello Award</a> for "A-Side With the Highest Sales".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[123]</a></sup> In 2001, "Hey Jude" was inducted into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Recording_Arts_and_Sciences" title="National Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences">National Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences</a> Grammy Hall of Fame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, <i>Rolling Stone</i> ranked "Hey Jude" at number eight on the "<a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone%27s_500_Greatest_Songs_of_All_Time" title="Rolling Stone's 500 Greatest Songs of All Time">500 Greatest Songs of All Time</a>", making it the highest-placed Beatles song on the list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[125]</a></sup> Among its many appearances in other best-song-of-all-time lists, <a href="/wiki/VH1" title="VH1">VH1</a> placed it seventh in 2000 and <i><a href="/wiki/Mojo_(magazine)" title="Mojo (magazine)">Mojo</a></i> ranked it at number 29 in the same year, having placed the song seventh in a 1997 list of "The 100 Greatest Singles of All Time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acclaimed_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Acclaimed-135">[126]</a></sup> In 1976, the <i>NME</i> ranked it 38th on the magazine's "Top 100 Singles of All Time", and the track appeared at number 77 on the same publication's "The 500 Greatest Songs of All Time" in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acclaimed_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Acclaimed-135">[126]</a></sup> In January 2001, "Hey Jude" came in third on <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a>'s list of the "100 Greatest Singles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[127]</a></sup> The Amusement &amp; Music Operators Association ranks "Hey Jude" as the 11th-best <a href="/wiki/Jukebox" title="Jukebox">jukebox</a> single of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[128]</a></sup> In 2008, the song appeared in eighth place on <i>Billboard</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s "All Time Hot 100 Songs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWomack2014391-73">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2006, <i>Mojo</i> placed "Hey Jude" at number 12 on its list of "The 101 Greatest Beatles Songs" (between "<a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Rigby" title="Eleanor Rigby">Eleanor Rigby</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Come_Together" title="Come Together">Come Together</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[129]</a></sup> On a similar list compiled four years later, <i>Rolling Stone</i> ranked the song at number seven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">ITV</a> program <i>The Nation's Favourite Beatles Number One</i> ranked "Hey Jude" number one in the show's countdown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Auctioned_lyrics">Auctioned lyrics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Auctioned lyrics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Julian Lennon discovered that "Hey Jude" had been written for him almost 20 years after McCartney composed the song. He recalled of his and McCartney's relationship: "Paul and I used to hang about quite a bit – more than Dad and I did. We had a great friendship going and there seems to be far more pictures of me and Paul playing together at that age than there are pictures of me and my dad."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECross2005367_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECross2005367-12">[12]</a></sup> In 1996, Julian paid £25,000 for the recording notes to "Hey Jude" at an auction. He spent a further £35,000 at the auction, buying John Lennon memorabilia. John Cousins, Julian Lennon's manager, stated at the time: "He has a few photographs of his father, but not very much else. He is collecting for personal reasons; these are family heirlooms if you like."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, the original handwritten lyrics for the song were nearly auctioned off at <a href="/wiki/Christie%27s" title="Christie's">Christie's</a> in London. The sheet of notepaper with the scrawled lyrics had been expected to fetch up to £80,000 at the auction, which was scheduled for 30 April 2002. McCartney went to court to stop the auction, claiming the paper had disappeared from his West London home. Richard Morgan, representing Christie's, said McCartney had provided no evidence that he had ever owned the piece of paper on which the lyrics were written. The courts decided in McCartney's favour and prohibited the sale of the lyrics. They had been sent to Christie's for auction by Frenchman Florrent Tessier, who said he purchased the piece of paper at a street market stall in London for £10 in the early 1970s. In the original catalogue for the auction, Julian Lennon had written, "It's very strange to think that someone has written a song about you. It still touches me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cover_versions_and_performances">Cover versions and performances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cover versions and performances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Hey Jude" was one of the few Beatles songs that <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a> covered, when he rehearsed the track at his 1969 <a href="/wiki/From_Elvis_in_Memphis#Recording" title="From Elvis in Memphis">Memphis sessions</a> with producer <a href="/wiki/Chips_Moman" title="Chips Moman">Chips Moman</a>, a recording that appeared on the 1972 album <i><a href="/wiki/Elvis_Now" title="Elvis Now">Elvis Now</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2004132.2C_147.2C_212_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2004132.2C_147.2C_212-143">[134]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medley_(music)" title="Medley (music)">medley</a> of the Beatles' "<a href="/wiki/Yesterday_(Beatles_song)" title="Yesterday (Beatles song)">Yesterday</a>" and "Hey Jude" was included on the 1999 reissue of Presley's 1970 live album <i><a href="/wiki/On_Stage_(Elvis_Presley_album)" title="On Stage (Elvis Presley album)">On Stage</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1968, R&amp;B singer <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Pickett" title="Wilson Pickett">Wilson Pickett</a> released a cover recorded at <a href="/wiki/Muscle_Shoals_Sound_Studio" title="Muscle Shoals Sound Studio">Muscle Shoals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFletcher2016141.E2.80.93148_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFletcher2016141.E2.80.93148-145">[136]</a></sup> with guitar from a young <a href="/wiki/Duane_Allman" title="Duane Allman">Duane Allman</a>, who recommended the choice to Pickett.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoe200881.E2.80.9387_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoe200881.E2.80.9387-146">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a> commented, "I remember hearing [it] and calling either <a href="/wiki/Ahmet_Ertegun" title="Ahmet Ertegun">Ahmet Ertegun</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tom_Dowd" title="Tom Dowd">Tom Dowd</a> and saying, 'Who's that guitar player?'...To this day, I've never heard better rock guitar playing on an R&amp;B record. It's the best."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[138]</a></sup> Pickett commented on the outro that "people were going crazy" and session musician <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Johnson_(musician)" title="Jimmy Johnson (musician)">Jimmy Johnson</a> said that the Allman's solo "created <a href="/wiki/Southern_rock" title="Southern rock">Southern rock</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFletcher2016141.E2.80.93148_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFletcher2016141.E2.80.93148-145">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/%22Weird_Al%22_Yankovic" title='"Weird Al" Yankovic'>"Weird Al" Yankovic</a> included "Hey Jude" in his first polka medley, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polkas_on_45" title="Polkas on 45">Polkas on 45</a>", from his 1984 album <i><a href="/wiki/%22Weird_Al%22_Yankovic_in_3-D" title='"Weird Al" Yankovic in 3-D'>"Weird Al" Yankovic in 3-D</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Katy_Perry" title="Katy Perry">Katy Perry</a> performed a cover of the song as part of the 2012 <a href="/wiki/MusiCares" title="MusiCares">MusiCares</a> Person of the Year concert honouring McCartney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Paul McCartney sang the song in the closing moments of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics_opening_ceremony" title="2012 Summer Olympics opening ceremony">opening ceremony</a> of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 Summer Olympics</a> hosted in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. On 4 August 2012, McCartney led the crowd in a rendition of "Hey Jude" while watching cycling at the velodrome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_Harrison" title="George Harrison">George Harrison</a><span class="nowrap"> </span>– <a href="/wiki/Backing_vocalist" title="Backing vocalist">backing vocal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead_guitar" title="Lead guitar">lead guitar</a>, handclaps</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a><span class="nowrap"> </span>– backing vocal, <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_guitar" title="Acoustic guitar">acoustic guitar</a>, handclaps</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a><span class="nowrap"> </span>– <a href="/wiki/Lead_vocalist" title="Lead vocalist">lead vocal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_guitar" title="Bass guitar">bass guitar</a>, handclaps</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ringo_Starr" title="Ringo Starr">Ringo Starr</a><span class="nowrap"> </span>– backing vocal, <a href="/wiki/Drum_kit" title="Drum kit">drums</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tambourine" title="Tambourine">tambourine</a>, handclaps</li>
<li>Uncredited 36-piece orchestra<span class="nowrap"> </span>– 10 <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violins</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">violas</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cellos</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Double_bass" title="Double bass">double basses</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flutes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinets</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Bass_clarinet" title="Bass clarinet">bass clarinet</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Bassoon" title="Bassoon">bassoon</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Contrabassoon" title="Contrabassoon">contrabassoon</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpets</a>, two <a href="/wiki/French_horn" title="French horn">horns</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Trombone" title="Trombone">trombones</a>, and one <a href="/wiki/Percussion_instrument" title="Percussion instrument">percussion instrument</a>; 35 of these musicians on additional backing vocals and handclaps<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[nb 10]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<dd>Personnel per <a href="/wiki/Ian_MacDonald" title="Ian MacDonald">Ian MacDonald</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005302.E2.80.93304_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald2005302.E2.80.93304-152">[142]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Lewisohn" title="Mark Lewisohn">Mark Lewisohn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisohn1988146-42">[39]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charts">Charts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Charts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weekly_charts">Weekly charts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hey_Jude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Weekly charts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
